PREFACE

THIS Edition of the Fifth Canto of the Kum√°rasambhavam is meant to meet the requirements of the students of the Intermediate classes. In it they will find the text as well as annotations on it and thus they will be saved the necessity of going for a bigger edition containing the whole of the poem.

The notes contain almost all that is needed for a student and if read with proper care they will prove of great advantage to him. Those who are regular students in some college should not neglect the lectures of their teachers, but should regard these notes as a supplement to them. Only then they can make the right use of these notes. As to the private candidates, they can safely depend on these notes for their success in the examination.

To acquaint the students with K√°lid√°sa and his poem an introduction has been added to the notes, which contains general as well as critical remarks.

The book has been brought out in a hurry, so it is quite possible that some mistakes of printing or otherwise may have escaped notice. For the pointing out of them we shall be thankful to the reader and corrections will be made in the next edition.

The author feels grateful to Kale’s edition of the Kum√°rasambhavam, on which is based the text and which he has consulted while writing these notes.

DEHRA DUN,

**August 1, 1935. **

MAHENDRA PRATAP SHASTRI

INTRODUCTION

_________

1. KALIDASA ‚ÄîTHE AUTHOR.

It is a matter of great pity that we possess practically no (1) His Life. trustworthy account of the presonal life of K√°lid√°sa, the greatest Indian poet. Like most of the other poets of Sanskrit he has observed complete silence about himself, and following his example, later writers too have abstained themselves from throwing any light on his personal history. Therefore the only sources to know something about him are, first, a number of traditions prevalent in the later period of classical Sanskrit, and, secondly, a few direct and indirect references contained in his works. The traditions associate too many interesting incidents with his name. At one place, for instance, he is said to have been a d√∫nce in his youth and later on to have got his enlightenment through the igrace of the goddess K√°l√≠, whence he is known as K√°lid√°sa. Another tale says that he owes his skill in poetry to his wife. A third one describes him as a shepherd by birth and a contemporary of King Bhojs of Dh√°r√° (1200 A. D.). One more asserts that he met with his death at the hands of a courtesan in Ceylon. But it is doubtful if any of these and other allied tales are of any real worth in studying K√°lid√°sa. What seems probable is that the incidents of some other K√°lid√°sa have been ascribed to the K√°lid√°sa of the Raghuvamsham and the Kum√°rasambhavam, who was the most famous of them. That there have been a number of poets of Sanskrit, who bore the name, K√°lid√°sa is proved by the following couplet of Rajashekhara, who knew at least three of them:‚Äî

‚Äò‡§è‡§ï‡•ã‡§Ω‡§™‡§ø ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡§®‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡•Ä‡§¶‡§æ‡§∏‡•ã ‡§® ‡§ï‡•á‡§®‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡•§
‡§∂‡•É‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§≤‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡•ã‡§¶‡•ç‚Äå‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡•á‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§¶‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡••‚Äô

In all this heap of fiction and legends only one thing is certain viz., that he was a contemporary of King Vikram√°ditya. But to identify this Vikram√°ditya is by no means an easy task. More than one king bore this name and several others used it as a title. There was one Vikram√°ditya who founded the Vikrama Era in 57 B. C., while Chandragupta II (413 A. D.) and Samudragupta (480 A. D.) of the Gupta dynasty had adopted the word as an epithet. Now which of these Vikram√°dityas was our poet’s patron is a puzzle, which still remains unsolved,

To come to the second source of information, namely, the evidence gathered from the references contained in his works we may hold it to be more reliable, though not infallible. On the authority of these references it can be said that he was a Br√°hmana by caste and a devotee of Shiva by faith. His vivid and exact description of Ujjayin√≠, Kashmere, the Him√°laya and some cities as well as parts of Nothern India, lead the reader to infer that he had been personally to all these places. He was fully familiar with the life at royal courts and seems to have lived, unlike Bhavabh√∫ti and M√°gha, prosperous, happy and popular life. Though fond of pleasures he was not licentious. He had a great regard for the fair sex and a noble idea of its chastity. This is warranted by the text of the Shakuntala. He was an all-round scholar, possessed of a sound knowledge of the Vedic principles, Upanishadic philosophy, Paur√°nika mythology, various Darshanika systems, grammatical and prosodical rules, medical science and astronomy.

As has already, been remarked, the problem of K√°lid√°sa’s date (2) His Date. is by no means an easy one, and it is nothing more than conjectures that we can take recourse to. To sum up the various theories on the subject we may divide them into two main groups. The adherents of the one hold that he flourished in the first century B. C. and was a contemporary of king Vikramaditys, the founder of the famous

Vikrama Era, while the followers of the other put him in the fifth or sixth century A. D.

The arguments advanced by the former group may be summed up as follows:‚Äî

** 1.** The state of the law of inheritance and the severe penalty for theft and other crimes, as referred to in the Sh√°

kuntala, point to the state of society and law as it existed prior to the Christian Era.

** 2.** Similarities in the works of Ashwaghoshs, a Buddhist philosopher and poet of the first century A. D. K√°lid√°sa lead one to place the latter before the former, because the possibility is that Ashwaghosha borrowed from K√°lid√°sa and not vice versa.

** 3.** A medallion discovered at Bhit√°, near Allahabad, on which is engraved a scene of two maidens watering plants and a king chasing a deer, and which is held to belong to the first century B. C. is considered by some scholars to represent the scene of the first Act of the Sh√°kuntala. Hence the drama must have been composed before it.

** 4.** Vatsabhatti, the author of the Mandasor inscription (472 A. D.) draws help from the poetry of K√°lid√°sa, therefore, K√°lid√°sa must have lived prior to the beginning of the fifth century A. D.

** 5.** Last but not the least, is the evidence of his style which is quite different from that of the writers of the medieval Sanskrit period. K√°lid√°sa’s style is simple, while the later writers find pleasure in embellished composition abounding in long compounds, difficult puns, etc.

It is on the basis of such evidence that scholars like Dr. Peterson believe that K√°lid√°sa stands near the beginning of the Christian Era, if, indeed, he does not overtop it.

The main arguments of the other group may be put forth thus:‚Äî

1. K√°lid√°sa’s language (specially his Pr√°krita) and style show a clear advance over those of the two earlier poets Bh√°sa and Ashwaghosha, who flourished in the first century B. C. and first century A. D. respectively.

** 2.** K√°lid√°sa’s works point to the state of perfection of Sanskrit literature which was attained during the reign of the Gupta kings, which period is known in the history as the “Golden Age for Brahmanism and Sanskrit.”

** 3.** There is a probable reference to Chandragupta II Vikr√°maditya (413 A. D.) in the title of the Vikramorvashiyam and to his son, Kum√°ragupta in the name of the Kum√°rasambhavam, and the remarkable allusion to the horse-sacrifice in the M√°lavik√°gnimitram may remind one of Samudragupta’s horse-sacrifice.

** 4.** The works of K√°lid√°sa seem to be describing the social, political, religious and economical conditions of the Gupta period.

** 5.** The allusion to the H√∫na kings in the Raghuvamsham suggests that K√°lid√°sa lived after their invasion of the Punjab, which is dated in the sixth century A. D.

From these arguments it must be clear that there is nothing conclusive and both the groups of scholars rely only on conjectures. In such circnmstances the most that can be said is that K√°lid√°sa lived before 634 A. D., because he has been mentioned by name in the Aihole inscription of that date and also by B√°·πáabhatta in his Harshacharitam, of the same time, or that he can safely be placed in the fifth century A. D. To say beyond that is open to serious opposition.

** (3)** His Works As many as forty-one books have been ascribed to the pen of K√°lid√°sa, but that is highly doubtful.Many of them are evidently unworthy of the name and seem to have been passed in his name by some bogus writers to win popularity for their compositions, or they belong

to some other K√°lid√°sa. Thus out of the long list only six are universally acknowledged as his own and some doubt is expressed about the genuineness of the seventh, viz., Ritusambaram. These seven may be classified as following:‚Äî

[TABLE]

** (4) His Art‚ÄîStyle, etc.** K√°lid√°sa is one of the most brilliant jewels in the diadem of goddess Saraswati and he is unquestionably the best poet of Sanskrit literature. If Bhavabhuti attempts to share with him the laurels of Sanskrit drama and Bharavi, M√°gha or Shr√≠harsha of Sanskrit K√°vya, there is none who can vie with him in both of these branchies of literature put together. His Reghuvamsham is one of the best of Sanskrit K√°vyas and the Sh√°kuntalam is far the best of Sankrit dramas. Though the traditional vere:‚Äî

‚Äò‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§¶‡§æ‡§∏‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§µ‡•á‡§∞‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§ó‡•å‡§∞‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§
‡§®‡•à‡§∑‡§ß‡•á ‡§™‡§¶‡§≤‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ò‡•á ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§æ‡§É‡••

is, so far as our poet is concerned, a compliment in the right direction, it is not in the use of similes alone, but in the excellences of poetry in general, that he excels all other Sanskrit posts. Unlike the compositions of B√°nabhatta and Dan·∏çin, in his works aentiment predominates the outer embellishments, and in contrast with Bh√°sa and Ashwaghosha, his language and style are quite in conformity with his noble imagination. He is the master of language as well as of poetic fancy.

His style is simple, pure and chaste. It is brief as well as clear. The language flows without interruption and long compounds, involved construction and difficult puns are absent

from it. It is easy but not without due ornamentation (See Kum√°rasambhavam, Canto 5‚Äî verses 4, 18 and 78) He does not use any redundant particles or other words, which are used so frequently in the epics in order to complete the number of syllables prescribed for a stanza. It is difficult to find out any words in his composition of verse which do not contribute something or other to their meaning and do not help in advancing the story farther. Thus his poetry is free from both the loose construction of the epics and the affected compositions of the later writers. For this he can be compared to Virgil, the immortal author of Aeneid. His style is of the Vaidarbha class, which consists of firmness and evenness of sound, perspicusty, uniformity, grace, tenderness, clearness of meaning, dignity of epithets. vigour, elegance and the use of metaphorical exoressions. In accordance with the spirit of this style he prefers suggestion to elaboration.

One distinctive feature of K√°lid√°sa’s poetry is his love of nature. It is his vivid and life-like descriptions of natural scenery and natural objects, such as the Him√°laya, M√°nasa lake, ocean, the Ganges, etc., which have won for him the title of ‚ÄúPoet of Nature‚ÄùHis descriptive powers are remarkable and while describing nature he strikes us as a part and parcel of it. One thing worth noticing in this connection is that he ascribes human feelings and even actions to insnimate objects of nature, which makes the scene very effective. (See Kum√°ra 5‚Äî17.25). This is known in Euglish as ‚ÄúPathetic fallacy‚Äùand, in this respect, he may be compared to Wordsworth of English literature.

K√°lid√°sa has also succeeded in entering deep into human heart. His observation of its workings and feelings is remarkable and the manner in which he gives vent to them has added

much to his glory. In the words of Humboldt “Tenderness in the expression of feelings and richness of creative fancy have assigned to him his lofty place among the poets of all nations.” (See Kamara 5‚Äî1, 26, 67 and 58.) His poetry is also profusely interspersed with the statement of “Universal truth”. (See Kumara 5‚Äî45, 64, 75, 82 and 86). This shows his correct reading and vast experience of the workings of human mind and nature surrounding it.

Of figures of speech, simile is his favourite and his poetry abounds in exceptionally charming use of it. (See Kumara 5‚Äî52, 61.) Bat he does not seem to have neglected other important figures of sense. Thus he is equally rich in the employment of fancy (‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ) (see Kumara 5‚Äî25, 27 and 41). corroboration (‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏) (See Kumara 5‚Äî5.31 and 64) and illustration (‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§) (see Kumara 5‚Äî4), and one should not be taken by surprise to see that he also uses the figures of sound whenever suitable for his text and this he does with great skill. Of the figures of sound alliteration (‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∏) and repetition of syllables (‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§ï) are most common. Thus, one can say that K√°lid√°sa is a master of sense as well as of words.

These qualities have won for him an unqualified praise from Indian as well as foreign critics and poets. In India he is regarded as “The lord of poets” (‡§ï‡§µ‡§ø‡§ï‡•Å‡§≤‡§ó‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å)* and his poetry as sweet as ‚Äúhoney-laden flowers.‚Äù‚Ä† He is an unrivalled poet ‚Ä† and his Sh√°

kuntala is the most charming of dramas ¬ß

Sir William Jones, who was the first to translate the Sh√°

‚ÄúWouldst thou the young year‚Äôs blossoms and the fruits of its decline,

And all by which the soul is charmed, enraptured, feasted, fed?

II. THE KUMARASAMBHAVAM‚ÄîTHE POEM.

(1) The Poem‚Äî

General remarke. The Kum√°rasmbhavam is a Mah√°kavya. It is divided into cantos, its hero is a divine personage, possessing the qualities of a Dh√≠rod√°tta hero, its predominant sentiment is ‡§∂‡•É‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞ having ‡§ï‡§∞‡•Å‡§£ and ‡§∂‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§ as its accessories; its plot is mythological, it contains descriptions of cities, season, natural scenery, etc. Thus it fulfils the conditions laid down in the definition of a Mah√°k√°vya.

The size of the Kum√°rasambhavam is a matter of controversy. Some recensions of the poem consist of 17 cantos while some scholars say that it has 22 cantos. But the general belief is that only the first eight (1‚Äî8) cantos are from the pen of K√°lid√°sa and the rest were interpolsted by some of the writers afterwards. Mallin√°tha, the well-known commentator has annotated only upto the eighth canto, and the main incident of the poem vis., the birth of Kurm√°ra, only potential also has been reached in the eighth canto, so the story may

be regarded as complete after it. The contents of the later contos have little bearing on the main theme, and their style also convincingly indicates that cantos 9‚Äî17 are not from the pen of the writer of the first eight cantos.

For the theme of the Kum√°rasambhavam, Kalid√°sa has chosen a story of divine beings, which provides him an opportunity to take the loftiest flights of imagination. The scene of incidents is the grand Him√°laya, which, with its beautiful valleys and slopes, affords an occasion for describing natural scenes. The descriptions of P√°rvat√≠, a young, beautiful maiden full of yearning to win over Shiva, have been completed with great skill. Her dismay at the defeat of K√°madeva and the following determination to win over Shiva by means of severe austerities reveal that Kalid√°sa had powers to see into the emotional side of Parvati’s heart. The honesty and sincerity with which P√°rvat√≠ practises penance have been disclosed with remarkable success, and the dialogue between an ascetic and Parvati is one of the best pieces of its kind in Sanskrit poetry. Turning to the other incidents of the poem, no other poet would have been able to do so much justice to the imaginary attack of K√°ma on Shiva, the victory of the latter upon the former and the pitiable lamentation of the bereaved Rati. All this combined with the inherent qualities of K√°lid√°sa’s pen has achieved for the poem a high place in the line of the Sanskrit Mah√°k√°vyas, and one feels fully in concordance with Dr. Ryder in thinking that ‚Äúthere are passages in the birth of the War-God of a piercing beauty which the world can never let die.

** The Source of the Poem**K√°lid√°sa has generally taken the theme of his poems from the ancient stock of stories, the Meghad√∫tam and the M√°lavik√°gnimitram being the only exceptions. Some of his works deal with historical stories while others with mythological legends. The

Kum√°rasambhavam belongs to the latter class as the story of Shiva and P√°rvati is only a mythological one. It is very ancient and is mentioned in a number of Pur√°

nas, but none of them has done so much justice to it as the Shiva Pur√°na. K√°lid√°sa also seems to have borrowed the plot for his poem from this Pur√°na, but after borrowing the bare outlines, he, with the help of his poetical genius and rich fancy has given the story a new aspect.

** (3) The Title.*As has been remarked the chief incident of the story is the birth of Kum√°ra, an issue of Shiva and P√°rvat√≠, so the poem has been named as Kum√°rasambhavsm." This name can be derived as ‡§ï‡•Å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡•á‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§É(‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§É) ‡§ï‡•Å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§É, ‡§∏ ‡§è‡§µ‡§≠‡•á‡§¶‡•ã‡§™‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§Ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•çi e.,*the birth of Kum√°ra; the name denotes the poetry describing it by" the transference of an epithet,, or, ‡§ï‡•Å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§É ‡§ï‡•Å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§É, ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§ï‡§æ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§§‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§Ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç i.e, the poem in which the birth of Kum√°ra has been described will be called Kum√°rsambhavam.

For the benefit of students we give below a summary of (4) The Story. the story of the first eight cantos in English:‚Äî

Canto 1.‚Äî At the outset the reader meets with a graphic description of Him√°laya, the lord of mountains. From his wife Men√°he had a son and a daughter, named Main√°ka and P√°rvati respectively. The latter is mentioned to have been in her previous birth Sati, the daughter of Daksha and the wife of Shiva. Then follows a description of P√°rvati’s childhood and youth. When she is of marriageable age, her father is informed by N√°rada, the ever-travelling sage, that she would be married to Shiva, Hearing this, Him√°laya sends his daughter with her two friends to wait upon Chiva, who had adopted the life of an ascetic after the death of his wife Sat√≠.

*** Canto 2*.‚Äî**Simultaneously with this the gods were being very much harassed in heaven by a demon, named T√°raka, to get rid of whom they go to Brahm√°. They relate all their troubles to him and in reply are told that the only person who could rescue the world from the clutches of this demon would be the son of Shiva. At this the gods think of the marriage of Shiva and are informed by Brahm√°that P√°rvat√≠, who was waiting upon Shiva, was the only suitable match for Shiva, and that the son got through their marriage will serve their purpose.

  • Canto 3.‚Äî* Then to create the passion of love in the heart of Shiva, Indra calls on K√°madeva, who accompanied by Vasant (the Vernal Season) as his helper, sets out on his mission. The nature surrounding Shiva, all of a sudden, puts on the garb of the spring season, all its objects, animate or inanimate, are filled with the passion of love. Shiva too for a while seems to be yielding to it, but very shortly consciousness is awakened in him. He holds K√°ma responsible for the disturbance, and being angry with him, reduces him to ashes by means of the fire coming forth from his third eye. Suspecting danger, Him√°laya takes away his daughter and the plan of the gods seems to have been frustrated for the time being.

*** Canto 4.*‚Äî**K√°ma’s sad end causes great grief to his wife Rat√≠ who had accompanied him in his adventure. She weeps biterly and piteously and decides to breathe her last by burning herself with the body of her beloved. But an incorporeal speech pacifies her by giving an assurance that her separation from K√°ma was only temporary and that she would again be united with him.

*** Canto 5.*‚Äî**P√°rvat√≠, who had fallen in love with Shiva and whom she wanted to marry, seeing her object undone by the

demise of K√°ma, decides to win over her beloved by means of severe penance. Her mother dissuades her but she turning a deaf ear to her and taking permission of her father goes to a peak of the Him√°laya to practise austerities. She repairs to the place with her friends and commences her penance with all seriousness. Taking the vow of asceticism, she puts –æ–ø the dress of ascetics, and not minding the severity of elements, continues to mortify her body. She becomes renowned as an ideal ascetic and sages from far and near come to visit her. One day she is called on by a young ascetic,who was none but Shiva in disguise. First he pays a befitting tribute to the pious life of P√°rvat√≠and then asks her the reason of her penance and knowing it to be to win over Shiva as her husband, he begins to point out the various defects of Shiva, such as his fondness for inauspicious things, his unknown birth, his poverty, etc., and expects P√°rvati to give up her determination. But P√°rvat√≠, true to her sincerity, gives a suitable reply to all his arguments and gives a decisive proof of her adherence to Shiva. At this the visitor appears in his real form and declares himself to be her slave. This fills P√°rvat√≠’s heart with great joy.

  • Canto 6.*‚Äî

Being advised hy P√°rvat√≠ to procure her father’s approval, Shiva despatches the Seven Sages to Him√°laya for the purpose. They are received at his capital with proper ceremony, and, on being asked by Him√°laya, they disclose the object of their visit. Him√°laya is too glad to hear it and readily gives his consent to the proposal. The sages after fixing the day for marriage return to Shiva and preparations for it are forthwith undertaken.

  • Canto 7.‚Äî*

Due festivities begin at the capital of Himal√°ya and preliminary rites in connection with the marriage are performed. On the fixed day,. Shiva with all his atten-

dants and gods reachas to‚Äö place‚Äö of Him√°laya, where the party is received with due honour. The procesion of the bridegroom is enjoyed withparticular interest by theinhabitantsof the town. then the marrige ceremony is performed, and it being over the guest depart, butShiva stays to live there.

  • Canto 8.*‚Äî

This canto open with a description of love affairs of the newly married couple. After staying at his father-in-law’s house fer one month, Shiva with P√°rvat√≠leaves the place for a honeymoon trip, during which he pays visits to varions places of interest. On reaching Gandham√°dana, he select it for his residence and lives there with his consort in continuous pleasures. One night they drink wine and spend the night in connubial pleasures.

‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä‡•§ ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§∏ ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§™‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ, ‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ω‡§™‡§ø ‡§π‡§∞‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§É‡•§ ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ ‡§∏ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•Å‡§≠‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç, ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§ï‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§£‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç, ‡§ó‡§ú‡§æ‡§ú‡§ø‡§®‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç, ‡§∂‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∂‡§æ‡§®‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç, ‡§µ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§µ‡§æ‡§π‡§®‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç, ‡§¶‡•á‡§π‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§û‡•ç‡§ö ‡§ï‡§•‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§ö‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§§‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•§ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§ï‡§¶‡•ã‡§∑‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∞‡•ã‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§É ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§É, ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§¶‡•ã‡§∑‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§∂‡§ø‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§® ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§ö ‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§¨‡§ü‡•Å‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§∞‡§™‡§ø ‡§¶‡•ã‡§∑‡§ï‡§•‡§®‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Å‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§§‡§§‡•ã ‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•á, ‡§™‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡•Ä ‡§∂‡§ø‡§µ‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§π ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§û‡•ç‡§ö ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç, ‡§Ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§ó‡§§‡§ï‡•ç‡§≤‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡•§

‡§Æ‡§≤‡•ç‡§≤‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§•‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡§®‡•Ä ‡§ü‡•Ä‡§ï‡§æ‡§É‚Äî ‡§§‡§•‡•á‡§§‡§ø‡•§ ‡§™‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§§‡•á‡§® ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡•ã‡§É‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§™‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§§‡§É‡•§ ‚Äò‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§™‡§∏‡§Æ‡•É‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‚Äî ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡•Ä‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§É‡•§ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§≠‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§•‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§π‡§§‡§æ ‡§≠‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§æ ‡§™‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§ï‡§ø‡§®‡§æ ‡§à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§≠‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§∞‡§•‡§æ ‡§≠‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§É ‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§§‡•ã ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§∞‡§• ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∑‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§§‡§•‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡§Ø‡•á‡§® ‡§Æ‡§®‡§∏‡§æ ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•§ ‡§ß‡§ø‡§ô‡•ç‡§Æ‡•á ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§∞‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§π‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§®‡§æ‡§≤‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§ó‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ö‡•à‡§§‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§π‚Äî ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø‡•§ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡•å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•Å ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∏‡•å‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§´‡§≤‡§æ ‡§∏‡•å‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•ç‡§≤‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§´‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§§‡§•‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡•§ ‡§∏‡•å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ‡§´‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•É‡§∏‡•å‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§≤‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡•§ ‡§®‡•á‡§ö‡•á‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§´‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§§‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•á ‡§µ‡§Ç‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§• ‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‚Äî ‘‡§ú‡§§‡•å ‡§§‡•Å ‡§µ‡§Ç‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§Æ‡•Å‡§¶‡•Ä‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§∞‡•å’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡•§

** PROSE ORDER:‚Äî** ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§≠‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§π‡§§‡§æ ‡§™‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§ï‡§ø‡§®‡§æ ‡§≠‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§∞‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡§Ø‡•á‡§® ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•§ (‡§§‡§•‡§æ) ‡§π‡§ø ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡•å‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§´‡§≤‡§æ‡•§

** ‚Äò‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡•å‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§´‡§≤‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§æ‚Äô**is a sort of universal saying meaning that beauty has for its fruit good fortune as regards the beloved, i.e., that alone is the real beauty which attracts one‚Äôs beloved and makes him love it; otherwise it has no meaning, howsoeverpraiseworthy it may be in the eyes of others.

** CHANGE OF VOICE‚Äî**‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§≠‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§π‡§§‡§æ ‡§™‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§ï‡§ø‡§®‡§æ ‡§≠‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§∞‡§•‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç‡§π‡•É‡§¶‡§Ø‡•á‡§® ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•á; (‡§§‡§•‡§æ) ‡§π‡§ø ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡•å‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§´‡§≤‡§Ø‡§æ (‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á)‡••‡•ß‡••

HINDI TRANSLATION:‚Äî‡§á‡§∏ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§Å‡§ñ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§æ‡§Æ‡§®‡•á ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§æ‡§Æ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ú‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§∂‡§ø‡§µ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§´‡§≤ ‡§á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•Ä ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§®‡•á ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞ ‡§∏‡•á (‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•Ä) ‡§∏‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Ä, ‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ø ‡§∏‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§¨‡§π‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§ï‡•á ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∞‡•á ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§≤‡•à‡••‡•ß‡••

** ENGLISH TRANSLATION:‚Äî**Parvati, baffled in her hopes by Shiva who, thus, burnt the mind-born (Cupid) in her presence, cursed her beauty in her heart: for beauty has for its fruit good fortune as regards one’s beloved. I.

** PURPORT IN SANSKRIT:‚Äî**‡§∂‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§® ‡§Æ‡§¶‡§®‡§¶‡§π‡§®‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ö ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•á ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§™‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§µ‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§¶‡§æ‡§∏‡§É ‡§∂‡§ø‡§µ‡§Æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡•Ä‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡§™‡•ã ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡•á‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§§‡§™‡•ã‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•å‡§∞‡•Ä‡§®‡•à‡§∞‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø‚Äî ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø ‡§∂‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§® ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•É‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§®‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡§æ ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§Æ‡§É ‡§≠‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∂‡§ø‡§µ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§Ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§®‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡•§ ‡§è‡§µ‡§û‡•ç‡§ö ‡§∂‡§ø‡§µ‡§Æ‡§® ‡§Ü‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•Å‡§Æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É ‡§∏‡•å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§®‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ,

‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Æ ‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡§É‡••‡•®‡••

**PROSE ORDER:‚Äî**‡§∏‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§§‡§™‡•ã‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§á‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•§ ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Æ ‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡§É ‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö (‡§á‡§§‡§ø) ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§•‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡•§

** NOTES:‚Äî‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§Ç‚Äî(‡§∏‡§Ç+‡§Ü+‚àö‡§ß‡§æ+‡§ï‡§ø‡§É)** Intense contemplation (of any particular object so as to identify the contemplator with the object meditated upon). This is the eighth and last stage of Yoga. Here it may also mean silence or a vow of intense devotion (for Shiva), or, a rigid rule of conduct, ‡§Ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‚Äî(‡§Ü+‚àö‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ+‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‚Äî‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡•ç‡§Ø taking recourse to. **‡§§‡§™‡•ã‡§≠‡§ø‡§É‚Äî**by means of austerities, the observance of which warrants all sorts of success. Cf. The

shloka of Manusmriti quoted above by Mallinatha. For purposes of the ‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•Å‡§ï ‡§≤‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞s it becomes ‡§á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ, e.g., ‡§á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§§‡§ø (‡§≤‡•ã‡§ü‡•ç) etc. **‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ‚Äî**otherwise i. e. without resorting to penances. ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§Ç‚Äî‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡•ã ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡•ç of that kind. That is, such love by which she could become Shiva’s wife. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Æ‚Äî‡§∏‡•ç‡§®‡•á‡§π‡§É love. According to Mallinatha women aspire for two things with regard to their husbands, viz., first, undiminished and constant love from them and secondly, longivity for their husbands. These two things are suggested respectively by ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Æ and ‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡§É ‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á (‡§Ö‡§µ+‚àö‡§Ü‡§™‡•ç+ ‡§Ø+ ‡§≤‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§≤‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á is obtained. It is Passive Pres. (‡§≤‡§ü‡•ç) Third Person Singular.

** ENGLISH TRANSLATION;‚Äî**Mena, hearing that her daughter, who had set her heart on Shiva, was determined to practise penance, clasped her to her bosom, and, warding her off from the great vow of ascetics, spoke (to her) 3.

‡§∏‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•á‡§ß‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•á‡§ß‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡•§ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø‡•§ ‡§π‡•á ‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•á! ‡§Æ‡§®‡§∏ ‡§à‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§É ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É‡•§ ‡§∂‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§É‡•§ ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§∂‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡§É ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡•§ ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§É ‡§§‡§™‡§É ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡•§ ‡§§‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•ã‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ñ‡§û‡•ç‡§ö’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§£‡•ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§É‡•§ ‘‡§§‡§µ‡§ï‡§Æ‡§Æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§µ‡•á‡§ï‡§µ‡§ö‡§®‡•á’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§µ‡§ï‡§æ‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§É‡•§ ‡§µ‡§™‡•Å‡§É ‡§ö ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡•§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø ‡§™‡•á‡§≤‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•É‡§¶‡•Å‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≠‡•É‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§™‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§π‡•á‡§§‡•§ ‡§™‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§™‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§® ‡§∏‡§π‡•á‡§§‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§§‡§ø‡§∏‡•å‡§ï‡•Å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§™‡§≠‡•ã‡§ó‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§§‡•á ‡§µ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§® ‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§§‡§™‡§É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§É‡••‡•™‡••

** PROSE ORDER:‚Äî**‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•á, ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø, ‡§§‡§™‡§É ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ, ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§™‡•Å‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ? ‡§™‡•á‡§≤‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§π‡•á‡§§, ‡§™‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡§®‡••‡•™‡••

** NOTES:‚Äî‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É‚Äî‡§Æ‡§®‡§∏‡§É ‡§à‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É** desired;wished; such as the mind would long for. ‡§à‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É is Past Part. from ‚àö‡§à‡§∑‡•ç ‡§ó‡§§‡§ø‡§π‡§ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§®‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‘to see.’ Here the Compound is of ‡§Æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç and ‡§à‡§∑‡§ø‡§§, in which the final vowel with the following consonant (‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç) of ‡§Æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç merges into the following word according to ‘‡§∂‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∑‡•Å ‡§™‡§∞‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§’ or, according to some it could be expounded as ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§æ (‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§£‡§æ) ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§§‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§æ+ ‡§á‡§§‡§ö‡•ç‡•§ In that case ‡§á‡§§‡§ö‡•ç will be added to ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§æ by the rule ‘‡§§‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§á‡§§‡§ö‡•ç’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•´-‡•®. ‡•©‡•¨ i. e. ‡§á‡§§‡§ö‡•ç is affixed to ‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ and other words in the sense of ’that is obtained or possessed by.’ ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§É‚Äî‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡•á‡§£ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡•å‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§æ ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É i. e., idols of deities such as Indrani, who can bestow good luck on you, Mena means to say that Parvati should worship those deities instead of trying to appease Shiva. ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‚Äîwhere? ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ is

formed from ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç by adding ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç to it and changing it into ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ by the rules ‚Äò‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ã‡§Ω‡§§‡•ç‚Äô‡§™‡§æ‡§£‡§ø‡§®‡§ø ‡•´.‡•©‡•¶.‡•ß‡•® and ‘‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡§ø’ ‡•≠. ‡•®. ‡•ß‡•¶‡•´. respectively.

**‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ…‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‚Äî**It implies excessive incongruity, and lit, it means ’where is the one and where is the other’ or ’how distant is one from the other’, or ’how unequal the two are.’ The repetition of ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ shows the great disparity between two things. Cf. ‘‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡§µ‡•ã ‡§µ‡§Ç‡§∂‡§É ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§§‡§ø‡§É’ ‡§∞‡§ò‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ç‡§∂‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•ß. ‡•® ‡•§ ‘‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§™‡§§‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§µ‡§É’ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ú‡•Å‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø ‡•ß ‡•¨.‡•§ ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§ï‡§Ç‚Äî‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§Ç yours; thine. ‡§§‡§µ ‡§á‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ First ‡§Ö‡§£‡•ç suffix is added to ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•ç by ‘‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•ã‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ñ‡§û‡•ç‡§ö’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•™. ‡•©. ‡•ß (‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•ç+‡§Ö‡§£‡•ç) and then ‡§§‡§µ‡§ï is substituted for it by ‘‡§§‡§µ‡§ï‡§Æ‡§Æ‡§ï‡§æ‡§µ‡•á‡§ï‡§µ‡§ö‡§®‡•á’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•™. ‡•©. ‡•©. (‡§§‡§µ‡§ï+‡§Ö‡§£‡•ç=‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç)‡•§ The alternative form, when ‡§ñ‡§û‡•ç is added instead of ‡§Ö‡§£‡•ç, is ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ Similarly are formed ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ï‡§Ç and ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç from **‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•ç‡•§ ‡§µ‡§™‡•Å‡§É‚Äî**body. Decline as ‡§µ‡§™‡•Å‡§É ‡§µ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•Ä, ‡§µ‡§™‡•Ç‡§Ç‡§∑‡§ø‡•§ ‡§µ‡§™‡•Å‡§É, ‡§µ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•Ä, ‡§µ‡§™‡•Ç‡§Ç‡§∑‡§ø‡•§ ‡§µ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ etc. ‡§™‡•á‡§≤‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç‚Äî‡§Æ‡•É‡§¶‡•Å tender. **‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§Æ‡•ç‚Äî**the flower of ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑ (‡§∏‡§ø‡§∞‡§∏ ‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Ä) is well-known for its softness and is often used as standard of comparison (‡§â‡§™‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®) to indicate that quality. ‡§™‡§¶‚Äî‡§™‡§¶‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡§Ç placing of feet; sitting. ‡§∏‡§π‡•á‡§§‚Äîmay bear. Potential (‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡§ø‡§≤‡§ø‡§ô‡•ç) of ‚àö‡§∏‡§π ’to bear, which is an ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•á‡§™‡§¶‡•Ä root. ‡§™‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§É‚Äî(‡§™‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞+‡§á‡§®‡§ø‡§É) ‡§™‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø‡•§ ‡§™‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø means wings, because they keep birds from falling; then, ‡§™‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•á ‡§™‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§É ‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§É those possessed of wings, i.e., birds. ** CHANGE OF VOICE‚Äî**‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•á!‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§É ‡§ó‡•É‡§π‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡§É ‡§§‡§™‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ, ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§ï‡•á‡§® ‡§µ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ö ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ (‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á)?‡§™‡•á‡§≤‡§µ‡•á‡§® ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡•á‡§£ ‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§π‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§§ ‡§™‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡§®‡••‡•™‡••

** HINDI TRANSLATION:‚Äî**‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø, ‡§Æ‡§® ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ö‡§æ‡§π‡•á ‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡§æ (‡§π‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•á) ‡§ò‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç; ‡§ï‡§π‡§æ‡§Å ‡§§‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§π (‡§ï‡§†‡•ã‡§∞) ‡§§‡§™ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§π‡§æ‡§Å ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ (‡§ï‡•ã‡§Æ‡§≤) ‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞! ‡§∏‡§ø‡§∞‡§∏ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã‡§Æ‡§≤ ‡§´‡•Ç‡§≤ ‡§≠‡•å‡§Ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§ï‡•á ‡§™‡•à‡§∞ (‡§∞‡§ñ‡§®‡•á) ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§§‡•ã ‡§∏‡§π ‡§∏‡§ï‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§™‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•á ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç‡••‡•™‡••

** ENGLISH TRANSLATION:‚Äî**In the house are such deities as your mind would long for. Child, how widely different are penance and thy body? The delicate Shirisha flower may bear (the stroke of) feet of a bee, but not that of a bird. 4.

** PURPORT IN SANSKRIT:‚Äî‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§æ‡§ö‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§ï‡§•‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á, ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§ø; ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•á‡§∂‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§ï‡§æ ‡§á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡•å‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡§æ‡§É ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§É ‡§§‡§æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡§Ø‡•à‡§µ ‡§§‡•á‡§Ω‡§≠‡•Ä‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ, ‡§Ö‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§Ø‡•§ ‡§§‡§µ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡•ã‡§Æ‡§≤‡§É ‡§§‡§™‡•ã‡§Ω‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§æ‡§®‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡§†‡§ø‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§® ‡§§‡•á ‡§¶‡•á‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•ã‡§¢‡•Å‡§Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡§É‡•§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø‚Äî**‡§ï‡•ã‡§Æ‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∏‡•Å‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ã‡§¢‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ï‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡§É ‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡•ã ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å ‡§ï‡§•‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§®‡•§ ‡§§‡§µ ‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡•Å‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§§‡§™‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§§‡•Å‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç, ‡§Ö‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§™‡§É ‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡§Æ‡••‡•™‡••

‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§ß‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡•á‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∂‡§æ‡§∏‡§§‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•Å‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç

** NOTES:‚Äî**‡§ó‡•å‡§∞‡•Ä‚Äî‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä known so because of herbrilliant complexion. ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§∂‡§§‡•ã‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§æ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§É‚Äî‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡•ã ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§É (‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§ï‡•Ç‡§≤‡§É) worthy of one. ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡•á‡§∂‡§É‚Äî(‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø+‡§®‡§ø+‚àö‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç+‡§ò‡§û‡•ç) intentness determination (to effect a purpose or attain an object). Instrumental Sing. of ‡§ó‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç (‡§ó‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å +‡§à‡§Ø‡§∏‡•Å‡§®‡•ç) comparative degree of ‡§ó‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡•§ Decline as ‡§ó‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç, ‡§ó‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡•å, ‡§ó‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§É‡•§ ‡§ó‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡§Ç, ‡§ó‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡•å, ‡§ó‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§∏‡§É‡•§ To form comparative and superlative degrees of adjectives ‡§§‡§∞‡•ç or ‡§à‡§Ø‡§∏‡•Å‡§®‡•ç and ‡§§‡§Æ‡§™‡•ç or ‡§á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§®‡•ç are respectively added by ‘‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§µ‡§ö‡§®‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§™‡§™‡§¶‡•á ‡§§‡§∞‡§™‡•ç ‡§à‡§Ø‡§∏‡•Å‡§®‡•å ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•´.‡•©.‡•´‡•≠‡•§ and; ‡§Ö‡§§‡§ø‡§∂‡§æ‡§Ø‡§®‡•á ‡§§‡§Æ‡§™‡•ç ‡§á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§®‡•å’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•´.‡•©.‡•´‡•´‡•§ ‡§ó‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§æ‚Äî‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ by the father*. Cf.* ‘‡§ó‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å ‡§ó‡•Ä‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‚Äå‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•å’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•Å‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‚Äî‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ (‡§¶‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ) ‡§Ö‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•Å‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‚Äî(‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å+‡§Æ‡§§‡§ø‡§É) ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∏‡§æ To whom consent or permission had been granted. ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å+‚àö‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ+‡§Ö‡§ô‡•ç) permission. **‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‚Äî**afterwards i.e. when Parvati had finished her penance. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å‚Äî‡§ú‡§®‡•á‡§∑‡•Å among the people. ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ‚Äî‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É (‡§ó‡•å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É) ‡§Ü‡§æ‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ (‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§Ø‡§æ) ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ after her name. Cf. ‡§ó‡•å‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∂‡§ø‡§ñ‡§∞‡§Ç, name of a peak of the Himalayas. ‡§Ü‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ is formed from ‚àö‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§ô‡•ç+‡§Ö, ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§ô‡•ç being replaced by ‚àö‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§û‡•ç by ‘‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§ô‡•ç ‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§û‡•ç ‡•®.‡•™.‡•´‡•™. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•+‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ç known. ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§Æ‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‚Äî(‡§∂‡§ø‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°+ ‡§á‡§®‡§ø‡§É) ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§É (‡§∂‡§ø‡§ñ‡§æ) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø one having a crest i. e., a pea-cock. ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§Æ‡§§‡•ç‚Äî(‡§∂‡§ø‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§®‡•ç + ‡§Æ‡§§‡•Å‡§™‡•ç) ‡§∂‡§ø‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§®‡§É (‡§Æ‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§É) ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§á‡§§‡§ø abounding in pea-cocks. This shows the absence of poisonous serpents and other ferocious beasts. ** ENGLISH TRANSLATION:‚Äî**Being permitted by her highly venerable father, who was satisfied with her determination worthy of her, Gauri resorted to a peak, abounding in peacocks, which afterwards came to be known by her name among the people. 7.

** NOTES:‚Äî‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§æ‚Äî‡§® ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É (‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§æ** whose determination was unalterable, i. e., of firm resolution, ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‚Äî(‚àö‡§π‡•É+‡§£‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç) which can be removed or given up. ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•á‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø a string of pearls of a necklace. An Adj. of ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç, the necklace. ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‚Äî(‡§µ‡§ø+ ‚àö ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§û‡•ç‡§ö + ‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡•ç ) ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ having given up. The compound which is ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø is an Adjective of ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§ï‡§≤‡§Ç and so is in Accusative Sing. ‡§™‡§Ø‡•á‡§æ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§∏‡§Ç‡§π‡§§‡§ø‡•§ ‡§™‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ß‡§∞‡§É‚Äî‡§ß‡§∞‡§§‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡§ß‡§∞‡§É (‚àö‡§ß‡•É ‡§™‡§Ø‡§∏‡§É (‡§¶‡•Å‡§ó‡•ç‡§ß‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø) ‡§ß‡§∞‡§É ‡§™‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ß‡§∞‡§É breasts. ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•á‡§ß‚Äî(‡§â‡§§‡•ç+ ‡§∏‡§ø‡§ß‡•ç + ‡§ò‡§û‡•ç ) height; elevation. ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‚Äî(‡§µ‡§ø+‚àö‡§∂‡•É‡•É+‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É) + ‡§Ö‡§™‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ò‡§ü‡§ø‡§§‡§æ broken or made less thick; shattered. Past Part. of ‚àö‡§∂‡•É‡•Éto tear to pieces. ‡•§‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ is changed to ‡§® and ultimately to ‡§£‡•§ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§π‡§§‡§ø‚Äî‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§Ø‡§µ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∂‡•ç‡§≤‡•á‡§∑‡§É Compactness. (‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§ï‡§≤ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ó‡§æ‡§¢‡§º‡§æ‡§™‡§®‚Äî‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Ä) ‡§™‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ß‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•á‡§ß‡•á‡§® ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§π‡§§‡§ø‡§É ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡§Ø‡•á‡§æ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•á‡§ß‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§∏‡§Ç‡§π‡§§‡§ø the compactness

of which was broken by the elevation of her breasts. It is also an Adjective of ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§ï‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§ï‡§≤‡§Ç‚Äî

bark; skin of a tree. ‡§¨‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‚Äî

tied; put on. Perfect of**‚àö‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç** to tie.

In this and the following three stanzas the poet describes how did Parvati adopt the various things, one after the other, necessary for a person practising penance.

** CHANGE OF VOICE:‚Äî**

‡§Ö‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•ã‡§≤‡§Ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≤‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‚Äå‡§®‡§Ç ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§¨‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§¨‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å ‡§™‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ß‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•á‡§ß‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§∏‡§Ç‡§π‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§ï‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§¨‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•á‡••‡•Æ‡••

** HINDI TRANSLATION:‚Äî**

‡§â‡§∏ ‡§Ö‡§ü‡§≤ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•Ä (‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä) ‡§®‡•á ‡§π‡§ø‡§≤‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•Å‡§à ‡§≤‡§°‡§º‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á (‡§õ‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§≤‡§ó‡•á) ‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§® ‡§™‡•ã‡§Ç‡§õ‡§®‡•á ‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§â‡§§‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§∞, ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡•Ä‡§® ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡§™‡§ø‡§∂, ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§ä‡§Å‡§ö‡§æ‡§à ‡§∏‡•á ‡§õ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§Ø‡§µ ‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•Ä (‡§§‡§®‡•Ä ‡§π‡•Å‡§à) ‡§™‡•á‡§°‡§º‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§õ‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§≤‡•Ä‡••‡•Æ‡••

** ENGLISH TRANSLATION:‚Äî**

She, whose determination was unalterable, having taken off her necklace, which wiped away the sandal-paste (on her breast) by its tremulous strings, put on a bark-garment, tawny like the morning sun (and) the compactness of which was loosened by the elevation of (her) breasts. 8.

** PURPORT IN SANSKRIT:‚Äî**

‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§≤‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§∑‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ò‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§ö ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§™‡•ã‡§Ω‡§®‡•Å‡§ï‡•Ç‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•§ ‡§Ü‡§¶‡•å ‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡•ã ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§É ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É, ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§æ‡§≤‡•á‡§Ω‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡§§ ‡§Ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§§‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§®‡§≤‡•á‡§™‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ú‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§æ‡§¶‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ö‡•ã‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡§ø‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§ï‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§ß‡•É‡§§‡§Ç, ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§ö‡§ï‡§æ‡§†‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•à‡§•‡§ø‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‡••‡•Æ‡••

‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•ã‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡•à‡§É
‡§ú‡§ü‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ‡§Æ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§

‡§® ‡§∑‡§ü‡•ç‡§™‡§¶‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡•á‡§µ ‡§™‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§ú‡§Ç
‡§∏‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§§‡•á‡••‡•Ø‡••

‡§Ø‡§•‡•á‡§§‡§ø‡•§ ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§®‡§® ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•à‡§É ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡•à‡§É‡•§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•å ‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡§≠‡•Ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡•å’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§∞‡•ã‡§π‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø ‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡§æ‡§É‡•§ ‘‡§á‡§ó‡•Å‡§™‡§ß‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§ï‡§ø‡§∞‡§É ‡§ï‡§É’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§É‡•§ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø ‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡§æ‡§É ‡§§‡•à‡§É ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡•à‡§É ‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§ú‡•à‡§É ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ‡§É ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‘‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡•Å ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡•å ‡§§‡•Å ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡•å’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§ú‡§ü‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§Æ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø ‡§™‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§ú‡§Ç ‡§™‡§¶‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§∑‡§ü‡•ç‡§™‡§¶‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∞‡§™‡§ô‡§ø‡•ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§è‡§µ ‡§® ‡§ï‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§∏‡§π ‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•á‡§® ‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø‡•§ ‘‡§§‡•á‡§® ‡§∏‡§π‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•Å‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•á’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§π‡§ø‡§É‡•§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§§‡•á‡•§ ‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§∂‡•ã‡§≠‡§§ ‡§è‡§µ‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É‡••‡•Ø‡••

** PROSE ORDER:‚Äî**‡§®‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•à‡§É ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•ã‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡•à‡§É ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç, ‡§ú‡§ü‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç (‡§Ö‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç)‡•§ ‡§™‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§ú‡§Ç ‡§∑‡§ü‡•ç‡§™‡§¶‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§è‡§µ ‡§® ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§§‡•á (‡§ï‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å) ‡§∏‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§ß‡§ø (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§§‡•á)‡••‡•Ø‡••

** NOTES:‚Äî‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡§Ç‚Äî‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§Ü‡§®‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•à‡§É‚Äî‡§∏‡•Å‡§∏‡§ú‡•ç‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡•à‡§É, ‡§Ö‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‚Äå‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•à‡§É** decorated. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß means ‘famous’ and ‘decorated.’ Cf. ‘‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•å ‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡§≠‡•Ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§§‡•å’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•ã‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡•à‡§É‚Äî‡§∞‡•ã‡§π‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡§æ‡§É (‚àö‡§∞‡•Å to grow + ‡§ï‡§É) which grow. ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø ‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡§æ‡§É ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•ã‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡§æ‡§É (‡§ï‡•á‡§∂‡§æ‡§É) which grow on the head i. e. hair. ‡§ï suffix is added by ‘‡§á‡§ó‡•Å‡§™‡§ß‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§ï‡§ø‡§∞‡§É ‡§ï‡§É’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•©. ‡•ß. ‡•ß‡•©‡•´‡•§ ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§Ç‚Äî‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Ç, ‡§∏‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç pretty. ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§Ç means ‘sweet’ as well as ‘agreeable.’ Cf. ‘‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡•å ‡§§‡•Å ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡•å’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‚Äîwas. Aorist (‡§≤‡•Å‡§ô) of ‚àö‡§≠‡•Ç ‘to be’ **‡§ú‡§ü‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§É‚Äî**by means of matted hair. The difference between ordinary hair and matted hair (‡§ú‡§ü‡§æ) is that the former are oiled, combed and decorated in other ways to enhance the beauty of the face, but no such care is taken in the case of the latter, which are allowed to grow in a natural way. **‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø‚Äî**also. The poet means to say that Parvati’s face being handsome looked as much pretty with matted hair

as with ordinary haireven ugly things can not mar the beauty of a really beautiful thing. This very idea is supported by the next two lines of the stanza. ‡§™‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§ú‡§Ç‚Äî(‡§™‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï+‚àö‡§ú‡§®‡•ç+‡§°‡§É) ‡§™‡§°‡•ç‡§ï‡•á ‡§™‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø‡§§ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø a lotus. ‡§∑‡§ü‡•ç‡§™‡§¶‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É‚Äî‡§™‡§ü‡•ç ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•á ‡§∑‡§ü‡•ç‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§É (‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§É) ‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É (‡§™‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É) swarms of bees. For the synonyms of ‡§∑‡§ü‡•ç‡§™‡§¶ Cr. **‘‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡•á‡§´‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§≤‡§ø‡§ü‡•ç‡§≠‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó ‡§∑‡§ü‡•ç‡§™‡§¶ ‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ø‡§É’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡§§‡•á‚Äî**shines or looks beautiful. Present of ‚àö‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç to shine. ‡§∏‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡•ç‚Äî‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç (‡§ú‡§≤‡§®‡•Ä‡§≤‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç; ‡§ï‡§æ‡§à‚Äî‡§π‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Ä) ‡§Ü‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§É (‡§∏‡§æ‡§π‡§ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç) ‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§É, ‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•á‡§® ‡§∏‡§π ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡•ç by its union with moss, **‡§Ü‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‚Äîcontact. The compound is ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§π‡§ø according to ‘‡§§‡•á‡§® ‡§∏‡§π‡•á‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•Å‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•á’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•®. ‡•®. ‡•®‡•Æi.e. the particle ‡§∏‡§π may be compounded with a noun in the Instrumental Case in a Bahuvrihi when there is equal participation in some action; in this case ‡§∏‡§π optionally becomes ‡§∏‡•§

For the idea contained in this stanza see “‡§∏‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§ú ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§¨‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•à‡§¨‡§≤‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç. ‡§Æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§®‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§π‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∂‡•ã‡§∞‡•ç‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§≤‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§§‡§®‡•á‡§§‡§ø‡•§ ‡§á‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§ï‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ ‡§¨‡§≤‡•ç‡§ï‡§≤‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä, ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡§ø‡§µ ‡§π‡§ø ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§®‡§Ç ‡§®‡§æ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡••” ‡§∂‡§ï‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§≤‡§æ ‡•ß. ‡•ß‡•Æ

** CHANGE OF VOICE:‚Äî**‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•à‡§É ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∞‡•ã‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡•à‡§É ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø, ‡§ú‡§ü‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ‡§Ç (‡§Ö‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø)‡•§ ‡§™‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡§ú‡•á‡§® ‡§∑‡§ü‡•ç‡§™‡§¶‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§∞‡•á‡§µ ‡§® ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•á (‡§ï‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å) ‡§∏‡§∂‡•à‡§µ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§™‡§ø (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á)‡••‡•¨‡••

** HINDI TRANSLATION:‚Äî**‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§∏‡§ú‡•á‡§π‡•Å

‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ö ‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Å‡§ï‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡•§
‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§ø‡§É
‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•ã‡§Ω‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§∏‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Ø‡•Ä ‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§É‡••‡•ß‡•ß‡••

‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•á‡§§‡§ø‡•§ ‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§≤‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§∞‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‘‡§Ö‡§ß‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§ß‡§∞‡•ã‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§§‡§É‡•§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡§æ‡§†‡•á‡•§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§≤‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ó‡•á‡§§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ú‡§®‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§É‡•§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ (‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡•á‡§£ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡•Ä‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡§§‡§®‡§∏‡§Æ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∞‡•Å‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§É‡•§ ‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Å‡§ï‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ö ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§§‡§É‡•§ (‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§ø‡§É) ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§≤‡§µ‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§Ö‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏ ‡§§‡§•‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É ‡§ï‡§∞‡§É ‡§™‡§æ‡§£‡§ø‡§É ‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§∏‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Ø‡•Ä ‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§π‡§ö‡§∞‡§É ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§É

**PROSE ORDER:‚Äî**‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§ß‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó-

‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Å‡§ï‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ö ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§§‡§É ‡§ï‡§∞‡§É ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§ø‡§É ‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§∏‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Ø‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§É‡••‡•ß‡•ß‡••

** NOTES:‚Äî‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§É‚Äî(‡§µ‡§ø+‚àö‡§∏‡•É‡§ú‡•ç+‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É) ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É** given up; left; removed. ‚àö‡§∏‡•É‡§ú‡•ç to create, changes its meaning when preceded by ‡§µ‡§ø‡•§ ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§É‚Äî(‚àö‡§∞‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡•ç+‡§ò‡§û) colour; the act of colouring. ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§É ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§É ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø, ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç of which painting had been given up, or from which the red colour, had disappeared. Before taking to the life of ascetism Parvati used to paint her lower lip with lac‚Äîdye, but, now she could not attend to such decorations. **‡§Ö‡§ß‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‚Äî**from the lower lip. ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§É‚Äî‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•á‡§™‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç scented cosmetic; application of unguents to the, body (especially after bath) ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‚Äî‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç reddened. ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•Å‡§£+‡§á‡§§‡§ö‡•ç ( ‘‡§§‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§á‡§§‡§ö‡•ç’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•´. ‡•®. ‡•©‡•¨) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É ‡§Ö‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡•á‡§£‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç reddened by the unguent of her breasts. While playing, the ball sometimes touched the breasts and so it was reddened by the paints applied to them. **‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Å‡§ï‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‚Äî**from the ball.Like the painting of lips Parvati had given up playing with ball also. ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§§‡§É‚Äî‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§É turned away. The hands were no longer employed for the two acts mentioned above. ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§ø‡§É‚Äî‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ö‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§É the fingers of which were pricked while plucking the tender blades of kusha grass. ‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§∏‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Ø‡•Ä‚Äî(‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Ø‡§É+‡§á‡§®‡§ø‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§∞‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Ø‡•ã (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç) the friend of the rosary of Aksha beads, i.e., the hand was employed in counting the beads. **‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§É‚Äî**was made. The poet means to say that on assuming the role of ascetics, Parvati substituted pluckingthe

kusha grass and counting of beads for painting of the lower lip and playing with the ball.

** CHANGE OF VOICE:‚Äî**‡§∏‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§ß‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Å‡§ï‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ö ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡•§ ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§∏‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Ø‡§ø‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä‡••‡•ß‡•ß‡••

** HINDI TRANSLATION:‚Äî**‡§õ‡•Ç‡§ü ‡§ó‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§∞‡§Ç‡§ó‡§®‡§æ ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ê‡§∏‡•á ‡§ì‡§† (‡§ï‡•á ‡§∞‡§Ç‡§ó‡§®‡•á) ‡§∏‡•á ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§≤‡•á‡§™ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§≤‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§π‡•Å‡§à ‡§ó‡•á‡§Ç‡§¶ (‡§ï‡•á ‡§ñ‡•á‡§≤‡§®‡•á) ‡§∏‡•á ‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•Å‡§è ‡§π‡§æ‡§• ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§â‡§∏‡§®‡•á ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§ô‡•ç‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ï‡§æ‡§ü‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ò‡§æ‡§Ø‡§≤ ‡§Ö‡§Ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§∞‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á ‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ (‡§ú‡§™‡§®‡•á ‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ) ‡§¨‡§®‡§æ ‡§¶‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡••‡•ß‡•ß‡••

** ENGLISH TRANSLATION:‚Äî**The hand, which was turned away from her lower lip, the painting of which had been given up, and from the ball reddened by the unguent of her breasts, was made by her such as the fingers of which were pricked by plucking the tender blades of kusha grass (and) the friend of the rosary of Aksha beads 11.

** PURPORT IN SANSKRIT:‚Äî**‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∂‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡§∂‡•ã‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡§æ‡§ß‡§∞‡•á ‡§≤‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§≤‡•á‡§™‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞, ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ã‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡•ã‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§û‡•ç‡§ö ‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Å‡§ï‡•á‡§® ‡§ö‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§°‡•§ ‡§§‡§™‡§É‡§ï‡§æ‡§≤‡•á ‡§§‡•Å ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡§ß‡§∞‡§∞‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•Å‡§ï‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§°‡§®‡§û‡•ç‡§ö ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡§∞‡§Æ‡§®‡§Ø‡•ã‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡•á‡§® ‡§™‡•Ç‡§ú‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∂‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§≤‡§µ‡§®‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞, ‡§Ø‡•á‡§® ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡•Å‡§≤‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§æ‡§É, ‡§Ö‡§™‡§∞‡§û‡•ç‡§ö ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§§‡•á‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§∞‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ú‡§ú‡§æ‡§™‡••‡•ß‡•ß‡••

‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡•á‡§§‡§ø (‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∂‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•à‡§É) ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡•ã ‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∂‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§≤‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§†‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•à‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡•á‡§∂‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡•à‡§É ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•Ä ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•ç‡§≤‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•§ ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡§æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§ï‡§∏‡•å‡§ï‡•Å‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§É‡•§ ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§â‡§™‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§ß‡§æ‡§Ø‡§ø‡§®‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§§‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•á‡§µ‡§≤‡•á ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§£‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§≤‡•á ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Æ‡•å ‡§è‡§µ ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•á‡§§ ‡§∂‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä‡•§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•á‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•Ä ‡§â‡§™‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ ‡§ö‡•§ ‘‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§É‡•§ ‘‡§â‡§ó‡§ø‡§§‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö’ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§ô‡•Ä‡§™‡•ç‡•§ ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§µ‡•á‡§µ ‡§∂‡§Ø‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ã ‡§® ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡•Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É‡•§

**PROSE ORDER:‚Äî**‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∂‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•à‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡•á‡§∂‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡•à‡§É ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ, ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§ß‡§æ‡§Ø‡§ø‡§®‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•á‡§µ‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§≤‡•á ‡§è‡§µ ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•á‡§§ ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•á‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•Ä ‡§ö‡••‡•ß‡•®‡••

** NOTES:‚Äî‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∂‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•à‡§É‡•§ ‡§∂‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‚Äî(‚àö‡§∂‡•Ä‡§ô‡•ç + ‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡•ç) ‡§∂‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§∂‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ** a bed. ‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•à‡§É (‚àö‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å+‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É) ‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡•ç‡§∞‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•à‡§É dropped. ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡•ã (‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç) ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§æ; ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç; ‡§∂‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡•à‡§É ‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø (‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§É) ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§æ‡§∂‡§Ø‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø, ‡§§‡•à‡§É which were dropped down in her rollings on her costly bed. ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡•á‡§∂‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡•à‡§É‚Äî‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§É ‡§ï‡•á‡§∂‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§™‡•à‡§É by the flowers of her hair, i.e, the flowers used for decorating the hair. ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‚Äî‡§ï‡•ç‡§≤‡•á‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ experienced pain. ‚àö‡§¶‡•Ç‡§ô‡•ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§™‡•á to suffer pain, to be sorry, is conjugated as ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§Ø‡§§ (‡§≤‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§¶‡•Å‡§¶‡•Å‡§µ‡•á (‡§≤‡§ø‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§¶‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á (‡§≤‡•É‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§¶‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü (‡§≤‡•Å‡§ô‡•ç) ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ is a particle which when used with a present tense or present participle, gives them a past sense. Cf. ‘‡§≤‡§ü‡•ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•á’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•© ‡•®. ‡•ß‡•ß‡•Æ‡•§ ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§ß‡§æ‡§Ø‡§ø‡§®‡•Ä‚Äî‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§§‡§æ, ‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§ß‡§§‡•ç‡§§ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§≤‡§Ç‡§§‡•ã‡§™‡§ß‡§æ‡§Ø‡§ø‡§®‡•Ä (‚àö‡§ß‡§æ+‡§£‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§É+‡§ô‡•Ä‡§™‡•ç) who uses her creeper-like arm as a pillow. Here the suffix ‡§£‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§É is added by ‘‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡•á‚Äå.’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•©.‡•®.‡•Æ‡•¶ and so it indicates the observance of a vow. ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§â‡§™‡§ß‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§ï‡•á‡§µ‡§≤‡•á‚Äî‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§£‡§∂‡•Ç‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á uncovered; bare. ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§≤‡•á‚Äî‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Æ‡•å on an earth-platform as opposed to a bed. ‘‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§ø‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§É’ ‡§π‡§≤‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ß‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•á‡§§‚Äî‡§∂‡•á‡§§‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ slept. Imperfect of ‚àö‡§∂‡•ã to sleep. ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡•á‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•Ä‚Äî(‡§®‡§ø+‡§∏‡§¶‡•ç+‡§ï‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§É+‡§ô‡•Ä‡§™‡•ç) ‡§®‡§ø‡§∑‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶ sat. ** PROSE ORDER:‚Äî**‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ö‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•ã‡§≤‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§∑‡•Å ‡§≤‡§§‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å ‡§ö ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•Ä‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§™‡§É ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§á‡§µ‡••‡•ß‡•©‡••

** NOTES:‚Äî‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§Ø‡§æ (‚àö‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ+‡§ï‡§É) ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•á ‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§§‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ, ‡§§‡§•‡§æ** by her, who was observing a vow. ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ö‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç‚Äî‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡•á‡§® ‡§ö‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç or ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Ç ‡§ö‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ö‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç sportive movements; coquetry. ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏ may be explained as‚Äîa form of feminine gesture considered as indicative of amorous sentiments.Cf. ‚Äò‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§®‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•á‡§∑‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§∏‡§Ç‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡•§’ ‡§∏‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ In idiomatic Hindi it is known as ‡§®‡§ñ‡§∞‡§æ or ‡§π‡§æ‡§µ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡•§ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•ã‡§≤‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç‚Äî‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•ã‡§≤‡§Ç (‡§ö‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§≤‡§Ç) ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç (‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§É)

‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•ã‡§≤‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç (‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ø‡§É) unsteady glances. Here ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç (‚àö‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡§É+‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É) is a noun and not a Past Part. ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç‚Äî‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•å ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§Ø‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡•ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç (‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø+‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§ö‡•ç) the two things; having two parts; a pair, ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§ö‡•ç is added by ‘‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•´.‡•®.‡•™‡•© ‡•§ The alternative form is ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§Ø‡§Ç‡•§ Similarly we have ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§Ø‡§Ç or ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§Ç (having three parts) from ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡•§ ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§∑‡•Å‚Äî‡§ï‡•É‡§∂‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å slender. ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä is fem. from ‡§§‡§®‡•Å‡•§ ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§®‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å‚Äî‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§®‡§æ‡§É, ‡§§‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å (‡§Æ‡•É‡§ó‡•Ä‡§∑‡•Å) female deer. **‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡•á‚Äî**with the two. It is Locative Singular. ‡§®‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§™‡§É‚Äî‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡§É a deposit. ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç‚Äî‡§¶‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç was given; was kept. The poet imagines that Parvati who was leading the life of asceticism could not use graceful movements and unsteady glances, hence she had deposited them with creepers and female deer respectively, who used them freely.

** CHANGE OF VOICE:‚Äî**‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡§æ‡§∏‡§ö‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§∑‡•Å ‡§≤‡§§‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•ã‡§≤‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç‡§ó‡§§‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡•á‡§Ω‡§™‡§ø ‡§™‡•Å‡§®‡§É ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•Ä‡§§‡•Å‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§™‡§Ç ‡§á‡§µ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä‡••‡•ß‡•©‡••

** ENGLISH TRANSLATION:‚Äî**By her, who was observing a vow, had been kept as a deposit to be taken back, the two things with the two, viz., her sportive gesture with the slender creepers and her unsteady glances with the female deer. 13.

** NOTES:‚Äî‡§Ö‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‚Äî‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§æ, ‡§®**

‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§®‡§≤‡§∏‡§æ without being slack. In (‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ+ ‡§á‡§§‡§ö‡•ç ) ‡§á‡§§‡§ö‡•ç is added by ‘‡§§‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§á‡§§‡§ö‡•ç‚Äô ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•´.‡•®.‡•©‡•¨ ‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ï‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‚Äî‡§π‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡•ã ‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§É ‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ï‡§É, ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç, ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§≤‡•ç‡§™‡§™‡§æ‚Äå‡§¶‡§™‡§æ‡§®‡•ç, little trees. The suffix ‡§ï‡§®‡•ç is added by ‘‡§π‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡•á’ ‡§™‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•´.‡•©.‡•Æ‡•¨ ‡•§ ‡§ò‡§ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§£‡•à‡§É‚Äî‡§ò‡§ü‡•å ‡§è‡§µ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡•å, ‡§§‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§£‡•à‡§É ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•É‡§§‡•à‡§É ‡§Ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§≠‡§ø‡§É with outpourings from breast-like jars. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§£‚Äî(‡§™‡•ç‡§∞+‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å+‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§ø ‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É‚Äî‡§Ø‡•Å of ‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å is changed to ‡§Ö‡§®‡•§ For a similar idea see ‘‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§π‡•á‡§Æ‡§ï‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§®‡§ø‡§É‡§∏‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•Å‡§É ‡§™‡§Ø‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§∏‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§É’ ‡§∞‡§ò‡•Å‡•¶ ‡•®.‡•©‡•¨. ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‚Äî(‡§µ‡§ø+‚àö‡§µ‡§ß‡•ç+‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§™‡•ã‡§∑‡§µ‡§§‡•ç reared up. ‡§ó‡•Å‡§π‡§É Name of a son of Shiva and Parvati, (popularly regarded as god of war,

because he leads the hosts of Shiva against the demon hosts). He is also known as Kumara, Skanda or Karttikeya. The name Guha may be derived as ‡§ó‡•Ç‡§π‡§§‡§ø ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§á‡§§‡§ø or ‚Äò**‡§∏‡•ç‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§ï‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡•ã ‡§ó‡•Å‡§π‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‚Äå‡§ó‡•Å‡§π‡•ã‡§Ω‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‚Äô**i.e, he is called Guha because he was reared in a cave. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‚Äî‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§Ü‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç) ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§Ø‡•à‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç who were born before;being first-born. Parvati being still unmarried Guha was not born as yet and all her affection for her children was devoted towards the small trees she reared up. ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§É ‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç (‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§≤+‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§û‡•ç) affection. ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç motherly affection; affection for one’s child. The compound may also be expounded as ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç (‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§™‡§¶‡§≤‡•ã‡§™‡•Ä ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§É)‡•§ ‡§® ‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø‚Äî‡§® ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø will not set aside. The idea is that even after the birth of Guha, Parvati will continue to love them.

** CHANGE OF VOICE:‚Äî**‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ï‡§æ‡§É ‡§ò‡§ü‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§£‡•à‡§É ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§, ‡§ó‡•Å‡§π‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§≤‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§® ‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡••‡•ß‡•™‡••

** HINDI TRANSLATION:‚Äî**‡§Ü‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§ ‡§π‡•Å‡§à ‡§µ‡§π (‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä) ‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•á ‡§Ü‡§™ ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§õ‡•ã‡§ü‡•á ‡§õ‡•ã‡§ü‡•á ‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§ò‡§°‡§º‡•á ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ú‡§≤ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡•Ä‡§Ç‡§ö‡§§‡•Ä ‡§•‡•ã; ‡§ó‡•Å‡§π ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§â‡§®‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á (‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§ï‡•á) ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞-‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¶‡•Ç‡§∞ ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§∏‡§ï‡•á‡§ó‡§æ‡••‡•ß‡•™‡••

**ENGLISH TRANSLATION:‚Äî**Free from lassitude, she herself reared up the small trees with outpourings from breast-like jars her motherly affection for which, being first born, even Guha would not be able to set aside, 14.

‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§§‡•à‡§É ‡§ï‡§≤‡§∂‡§ú‡§≤‡•à‡§∞‡§∏‡§ø‡§û‡•ç‡§ö‡§§‡•ç‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§™‡§Ø‡§∏‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§§‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡§æ‡§§‡§ø ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ò‡§ü‡§ú‡§≤‡•à‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§™‡•ã‡§∑ ‡§á‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É‡•§ ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§≠‡•Ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡•á‚Äî‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§ï‡•á‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§∞‡§Æ‡§µ‡§ø ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§® ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø, ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø ‡§§‡•Å ‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§®‡•á‡§π‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø‡••‡•ß‡•™‡••

‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§≤‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡§æ-
‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§ö ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§É‡•§
‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡§Ø‡§®‡•à‡§É ‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•Ç‡§π‡§≤‡§æ‡§§‡•ç

‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡§ø‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡•Ä‡§ú‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§≤‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§®‡•Ä‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§≤‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§æ‡§É ‡§ö ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Å‡§É ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§Ç‡§ú‡§ó‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Å‡§É‡•§ ‘‡§∏‡§Æ‡•å ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç‡§≠‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡•å’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§∞‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ ‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•Ç‡§π‡§≤‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§î‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•à‡§É ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§®‡§Ø‡§®‡•à‡§É ‡§®‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§É ‡§ï‡§∞‡§£‡•à‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•á ‡§∏‡§ñ‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§§‡§É‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§∏‡§π‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§§‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É‡•§ ‡§ï‡•á‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•à‡§∞‡•ç‡§®‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§É ‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•Ç‡§π‡§≤‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ω‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•á ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§ñ‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡•á ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§§ ‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§® ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§π‡•Å‡§É‡•§ ‘‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ô‡•ç ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•á’ ‡§á‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•ã‡§∞‡•ç‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç‡•§ ‡§á‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§ñ‡§≤‡•Å ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§¶‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§ø‡§™‡•Ä‡§°‡§®‡•á‡§Ω‡§™‡§ø‡§® ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Ä‡§§‡§ø ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§É‡••‡•ß‡•´‡••

** PROSE ORDER:‚Äî**(‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ç) ‡§ö ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§≤‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡§æ‡§É ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•Å‡§É ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ (‡§∏‡§æ) ‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•Ç‡§π‡§≤‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•à‡§É ‡§®‡§Ø‡§®‡•à‡§É (‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ) ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡§ñ‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§É ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§§‡••‡•ß‡•´‡••

** NOTES:‚Äî‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§≤‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É‚Äî‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡•á‡§É ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•á‡§® ‡§≤‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É (‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É)** fondled by being given handfuls of forest-grain. ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú means wild rice and such other grain as grow in forest of their own accord. Deer were generally reared up in this manner by maidens living in hermitages. Cf. ‘‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡§Æ‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§§‡§ï‡•ã‡•¶ ‡§Æ‡•É‡§ó‡§É’

‡§∂‡§ï‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§≤‡§æ‡•¶ ‡•™. ‡•ß‡•™ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‚Äîso much. ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•Å‡§É‚Äî(‡§µ‡§ø+‚àö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç+‡§≤‡§ø‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§É trusted. ‚àö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç ’to breathe’ means ’to trust’ when preceded by **‡§µ‡§ø‡•§ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡§ø‡§§‡§ø (‡§≤‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏ (‡§≤‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç (‡§≤‡•Å‡§ô‡•ç)‡•§ ‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•Ç‡§π‡§≤‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‚Äî**out of curiosity. ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•à‡§É‚Äî(‡§§‡§¶‡•ç+‡§õ‡§É‚Äî‡§à‡§Ø‡•ç) ‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§á‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø, ‡§§‡•à‡§É belonging to them (fawns). ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡•á‚Äî‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•á ‡§®‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á her own eyes. **‡§∏‡§ñ‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§É‚Äî**in the very presence esence of her friends. The fawns were so much tamed that they would not mind the presence of Parvati’s friends. ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§§‚Äî(‚àö‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ô‡•ç+‡§≤‡§ô‡•ç) Measured. Imperfect 3rd person Sing. of ‚àö‡§Æ‡§æ ’to measure.’ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡§ø (‡§≤‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•å (‡§≤‡§ø‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø (‡§≤‡•É‡§ü‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç (‡§≤‡•Å‡§ô‡•ç)‡•§ Some commentators, thinking it improper for Parvati to compare her own eyes with those of the fawns, construe the third and the fourth lines as‚Äî‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•Ç‡§π‡§≤‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•à‡§É ‡§®‡§Ø‡§®‡•à‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§É ‡§∏‡§ñ‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡•á ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§§ i. e., out of curiosity she measured the eyes of her friends before her (‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§É) with theirs. But this does not seem to be the idea of the author, who is expected to describe the eyes of Parvati and not those of her friends, and, secondly in that case we would expect ‡§∏‡§ñ‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§ø and not ‡§∏‡§ñ‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡•á ‡•§

** CHANGE OF VOICE:‚Äî**‡§Ö‡§∞‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¨‡•Ä‡§ú‡§æ‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§≤‡§ø‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡§≤‡§æ‡§≤‡§ø‡§§‡•à‡§É ‡§π‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•à‡§É ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•á ‡§Ø‡§•‡§æ (‡§§‡§Ø‡§æ) ‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡•Ç‡§π‡§≤‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§§‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•à‡§É ‡§®‡§Ø‡§®‡•à‡§É ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ö‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡§ñ‡•Ä‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§É ‡§Ö‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡••‡•ß‡•´‡••

** HINDI TRANSLATION:‚Äî**‡§ú‡§Ç‡§ó‡§≤‡•Ä ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ü‡•ç‡§†‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§¶‡•á‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡•á ‡§™‡§≤‡•á ‡§π‡•Å‡§è ‡§Æ‡•É‡§ó ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§á‡§§‡§®‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§•‡•á ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§µ‡§π ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡•Å‡§ï‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§∂ (‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•Ä) ‡§∏‡§π‡•á‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§â‡§™‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§â‡§®‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§Ü‡§Å‡§ñ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á (‡§Ö‡§™‡§®‡•Ä) ‡§Ü‡§Å‡§ñ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§®‡§æ‡§™‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•Ä ‡§•‡•Ä ‡••‡•ß‡•´‡••

**ENGLISH TRANSLATION:‚Äî**And the fawns, by being given handfuls of forest-grains, trusted her so much that, out of curiosity, she used to measure (her own) eyes with theirs in the presence of her friends 15.

** NOTES:‚Äî‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§∑‡•á‡§ï‡§æ‡§Ç‚Äî‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§É ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§∑‡•á‡§ï‡§É (‡§∏‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§Ç) ‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∏‡§æ, ‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç** who had taken a bath. ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§≠‡§ø‡§∑‡•á‡§ï‚Äî(‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø +‚àö‡§∏‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç +
